Frustrating Waste of Time This app might be helpful for somebody who is terrible at public speaking and afraid to practice in front of real people.  

However, if you are a professional who speaks for a living and you’re trying to improve your skills to the next level, it will be completely useless.

It had nothing to correct from my speech, so it just made up a bunch of things. For example, it said I had 38 filler words because I use the word “like”, “actually” and “so” and it didn’t consider the context of those words at all. This app is like a robot with no brain. In that sentence it would said “like” is a filler word, when actually it’s necessary for the meaning of the sentence. It also would say my use of the word “actually” there was a filler word. 

Along those lines it also said my words were vague because I used the word “something”, but again there is a place and time when the word “something” means something instead of just anything. See what I did there? That would be frowned upon by this app. 

It seems to be trained on certain words, being common filler words or common vague words, and then it just finds those in your speech and points them out whether it makes any sense in the context of your speech.

It is not intelligent enough to be helpful for somebody who is advanced speaking level.

The one redeeming factor is it allows your to practice your speaking skills with its little games, but with no viable feedback, you don’t really need an app for that. Just record yourself or practice in the mirror the old fashion way.

Elsa speaks app I found the app experience frustrating almost immediately. The AI avatar’s voice sounded robotic and unpleasant, and there was no option to change it. This is a significant drawback, especially for a product designed for frequent, long-term use.

The lesson structure also felt rigid. I attempted to change the topic from “landmarks,” but the app ignored the request and continued with the preset lesson. This lack of flexibility made the experience feel unnatural and limiting.

More concerning was the accuracy of the speech recognition. When I said “State Capitol of Texas,” the app repeatedly transcribed it as “state capital of Texas” and then incorrectly indicated that it was not a landmark. This kind of error undermines confidence in the app’s ability to provide reliable feedback, especially when distinguishing between commonly confused terms like “capitol” and “capital.”

Overall, I appreciate that there is a free trial, as it allowed me to evaluate the app before committing. However, after only a few minutes of use, I could not see myself using it consistently, let alone over the course of a full year.

大変使いづらい UI が大きく変更されました。次の様な点から大きく体験が悪化しています。もともとコンテンツの品質は悪かったですが、コンテンツを磨き込まずに UI だけ変えた結果、さらに悪くなりました。これまで BoldVoice が高いと思って我慢して3年間 ELSA を使ってきましたが、いよいよ乗り換えます。残念です。

・3D モデルで教師が表示されるようになり電池の消耗が激しくなった。ロードも遅くなりましたが。発音と口の動きが連動しているわけでもなく存在意義が不明。この 3D 教師が学習画面の半分を常に占拠しており消せない。学習コンテンツが見づらくなった。

・ボタンの配置に一貫性がなく操作しづらい。例えばスコアが悪い時はやり直すボタンが右だが、スコアが良い時はやり直すボタンが左に小さくでて、右にあるのは先に進むボタン。押し間違えが頻繁に生じる。また、やり直すボタンを押してもすぐにやり直せるのではなく、話すボタンを続けて押す必要がある。

・発音評価モードのベーシックと上級者向けのモード切り替えを覚えなくなったので毎度切り替えなければならない。

This critical flaw makes the app's primary purpose practically useless.
Worse, despite paying for the 'premium' version, I'm constantly bombarded with ads to pay for the app again. It's incredibly annoying and feels like a blatant disregard for paying customers. It doesn’t recognize pauses accurately One of my goals is to not only be a better public speaker, but to enunciate more clearly. When I saw an app like Orai existed, i felt it was a good opportunity to learn new skills that I would need. 

Unfortunately, the app has many problems with detecting pauses. Every single practice, it says the sentences are ‘too long.’ No matter how long you pause for (I’ve sat in silence for 5 seconds just to see if it recognized the end of a sentence), or even if you say ‘end sentence’ or ‘period’, it ignores this and treats every sentence as a single, long sentence.

This problem means that you end up stalling and restructuring your speech unnaturally, defeating the entire purpose of the app.

The idea of this app is good, and I support its mission, but the inaccuracy of the technology means that I cannot recommend it.

A bit of a scam, but not useless This app is a bit of a scam. Not worthless, but certainly not worth the subscription. The app guides you through vocal exercises, recording you reading a passage before and after each exercise, then shows you some metrics about pitch, pacing, and range, that you can use to gauge your own progress. The exercises are generally helpful, although as far as I can tell, they are just cycled  randomly regardless of your goals, prior results, or progress.

The “AI coach” feature is useless. It hallucinates filler words, and doesn’t really give advice tailored to your submitted recordings. It feels like random vocal advice.

Each exercise comes with 3 steps of written instructions, followed by a video demonstration of the exercise that you are meant to mirror. The instructions often don’t describe what the demonstrator is actually doing. I suspect the developers of the app input the demo videos to an LLM, asked it to write a three step set of instructions for each, then added them to the app without doing an QA due diligence. You can still mirror the video. But the fact that you have to ignore the textual instructions is insulting.

The value of this app is that it prompts you to do a daily series of vocal exercises, and provides you with metrics. They will probably improve your speaking voice.  But my dissatisfaction lead me to search online for other exercises that would focus on specific areas I wanted to improve. It turns out there are a lot them for free. Many are more effective.

A Cash Grab Disguised as an App – Rizz is a Complete Letdown I usually don’t write reviews, but my experience with the Rizz app was frustrating enough that I felt the need to warn others before they waste their time or money. This app is a textbook example of a modern bait-and-switch – promising innovative features, sleek design, and helpful tools, but hiding everything even remotely useful behind an aggressive paywall.

Let me break it down:

1. Absolutely No Free Functionality

The most glaring issue with Rizz is that there is nothing – and I mean nothing – useful you can do on this app without paying. The app markets itself as some kind of revolutionary social AI assistant or “rizz generator” (whatever that means), but the moment you open it, you’re greeted with pop-ups to upgrade or subscribe. You can’t even test the core functionality to see if it’s worth it. No trial, no basic version, no limited access. Just a brick wall with a price tag on it.

If you’re expecting even a basic feature set to be free – like a few messages, sample profiles, or basic prompts – forget about it. The “free” version exists in name only. It’s a glorified advertisement for their premium plan.

2. Shady Monetization Strategy

Rizz isn’t just subscription-based – it pushes you into premium tiers with outrageous pricing. You’ll be asked to cough up a monthly fee that’s comparable to full-featured productivity software like Notion, ChatGPT Plus, or Adobe tools – but for what? A chatbot that spits out generic compliments and recycled flirty one-liners?

There are constant prompts, misleading buttons, and sneaky UI tricks to get you to tap “Subscribe.” Some buttons look like regular features, but tapping them immediately leads to a payment page. It feels predatory, like the developers prioritized squeezing money out of users over offering any genuine value.

3. Misleading Marketing

The app’s description and promotional material make it sound like it will enhance your dating life with AI-powered conversation help, message templates, pickup lines, and smooth responses. But the reality is a watered-down, poorly executed chatbot with painfully limited use unless you pay. Even then, the premium features don’t deliver anything you couldn’t find online for free.

Their “AI” is no more intelligent than a random Instagram caption generator. And no, it doesn’t “learn from your conversations” – that’s just fluff to make the tech sound smarter than it is.

4. Poor User Experience

Aside from the paywall nightmare, the app itself is clunky and slow. Navigating between tabs is laggy, and half the time it takes several seconds just to load a simple menu. Notifications don’t work properly, and there are frequent crashes. The UI is overly flashy but unintuitive, with confusing layouts that seem more interested in looking “modern” than actually being usable.

5. No Value for Money

Even if you do decide to shell out cash, you’ll quickly realize how shallow the content is. There’s a small collection of recycled templates and generic messages that feel like they were scraped from Reddit in 2018. The AI doesn’t adapt, doesn’t learn, and doesn’t impress. If you’re looking for genuine help with conversations or flirting, you’d be better off reading a few free blog articles or watching YouTube videos – at least those have some depth and variety.

6. Support is Useless

If you think contacting support will help, think again. There’s no real customer service – just a chatbot with auto-replies. I reached out about the misleading “free” access and received nothing but canned responses and an unhelpful link to their FAQ. No refunds, no accountability, no dialogue.

⸻

Final Verdict: 1/5 Stars (And that’s generous)

Rizz is a perfect example of everything that’s wrong with mobile apps in 2025. It promises innovation but delivers frustration. It hides basic functionality behind an aggressive paywall. It pretends to be smart but ends up being generic. It offers no trial, no transparency, and no reason to trust it.

Unless you’re interested in paying a premium subscription just to test whether it’s worth anything (spoiler: it’s not), I strongly recommend avoiding this app. There are far better tools out there – many of them free – that actually help with communication, relationships, or self-confidence.

Don’t fall for the hype. Rizz is all marketing and no substance.

Unimpressed and Cringeworthy: The Corny Pick-Up Lines on the Rizz App Rating: ★☆☆☆☆

I recently gave the Rizz app a try, hoping to find some entertaining and clever pick-up lines to lighten the mood and spark interesting conversations. However, my experience with the app left me utterly unimpressed and cringing at the sheer corniness of the pick-up lines it offered. This app is nothing more than a cornball's paradise, and I cannot recommend it to anyone seeking genuine or witty interactions.

First and foremost, the pick-up lines provided on the Rizz app were embarrassingly cheesy and lacked any semblance of originality. It felt as if the developers had scraped the bottom of the barrel for tired and overused clichés. Instead of offering clever or creative lines that could potentially make someone smile or laugh, I was met with unimaginative and cringe-inducing attempts at humor.

Moreover, the pick-up lines were often ineffective and unrealistic in real-world scenarios. It's one thing to embrace humor and lightheartedness in an attempt to break the ice, but the lines on this app were so far-fetched and forced that they came across as insincere and even creepy. It felt like a desperate attempt to impress rather than a genuine effort to connect with someone.

Another glaring issue with the Rizz app was the lack of variety and customization in the pick-up lines. The app provided a limited pool of lines that quickly became repetitive and predictable. There was no option to personalize or tailor the lines to suit individual preferences or situations. This lack of flexibility made the whole experience feel monotonous and uninspiring.

Furthermore, the user interface and overall design of the app were subpar. It was clunky and unappealing, making navigation a cumbersome task. The app lacked any engaging or interactive elements that could have made the experience more enjoyable. It was clear that the developers focused more on quantity rather than quality, neglecting essential aspects of user experience.

Feels sus / scammy This review is based only on the sign-up, I never actually made it into the app. Two huge red flags:

1) Despite multiple opportunities to say how much the app subscription costs, it does  not indicate this anywhere. You’re expected to agree to a 5-day trial and then be charged through the app store. My assumption is they’re angling for maximizing first-month revenue. 

2) The sign-up process feels like every other self-help app: it starts out with perfunctory questions to prime you to imagine how it might help you and then rather than letting you try out the app first, they immediately convert you to a trial subscription (see above). In my experience with other apps, these initial questions are not conveyed on to the app, so the point of the questions is purely to convince you to convert to a subscriber, and not to actually be used for your benefit. (Having not used this app, I dont know for certain this is the case — but it has all the hallmarks of these poor quality self help apps)

Frustrating paywall before you can even try it I was really excited to try Gleam after hearing good things, but the sign-up process immediately killed my interest. Right from the start, it pushes you to subscribe before you’ve seen anything meaningful in the app. Yes, they offer a free trial, but it still requires entering payment info and enabling a recurring subscription that I’d have to remember to cancel.
I get that developers need to make money, but this approach feels like a money grab. If you truly believe in your product and want people to fall in love with it, why not let users explore the core features freely first—without tying anything to a recurring billing cycle? Here’s the advice in a nutshell: smile, act happy, don’t think bad things, don’t criticize people or be negative in front of them, say appreciative things.

It’s the vibe-coded app version of Dale Carnegie’s book. Has formatting issues. Typos.

I also would argue some of the advice given is genuinely poor and dated. The barista Chloe does not want you to address her by name because you read her nametag. Only “ownership” class people think that way. Hourly service workers generally find it invasive when customers speak with intense familiarity immediately. This is crackpot stuff designed to trick lonely or dysfunctional people into losing $70. 

I also really enjoyed all the reminders in the lessons that other people don’t care about you, don’t want to hear what you have to say, don’t care if they hurt you and you couldn’t even try to tell them because others will not listen as they  are inherently selfish.
